	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">John Locke</h1>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller,_National_Portrait_Gallery,_London.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG/220px-John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG/330px-John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG/440px-John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2056" data-file-height="3088" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller,_National_Portrait_Gallery,_London.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Locke's portrait by <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_Kneller" title="Godfrey Kneller">Godfrey Kneller</a>, National Portrait Gallery, London</div></div></div>
<p><b>John Locke</b> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a></span> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/ɒ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">ɒ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span></span>/</a></span></span>; 29 August 1632&#160;– 28 October 1704) was an English <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a> and physician, widely regarded as one of the most influential of <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> thinkers and commonly known as the "Father of <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">Liberalism</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Considered one of the first of the British <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricists</a>, following the tradition of Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, he is equally important to <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> theory. His work greatly affected the development of <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosophy</a>. His writings influenced <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, many <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish Enlightenment</a> thinkers, as well as the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American revolutionaries</a>. His contributions to <a href="/wiki/Classical_republicanism" title="Classical republicanism">classical republicanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal theory">liberal theory</a> are reflected in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Locke's <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">theory of mind</a> is often cited as the origin of modern conceptions of <a href="/wiki/Identity_(philosophy)" title="Identity (philosophy)">identity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Self_(psychology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Self (psychology)">self</a>, figuring prominently in the work of later philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, Rousseau, and <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>. Locke was the first to define the self through a continuity of <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>. He postulated that, at birth, the <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> was a blank slate or <i><a href="/wiki/Tabula_rasa" title="Tabula rasa">tabula rasa</a></i>. Contrary to <a href="/wiki/Descartes" class="mw-redirect" title="Descartes">Cartesian</a> philosophy based on pre-existing concepts, he maintained that we are born without <a href="/wiki/Innatism" title="Innatism">innate ideas</a>, and that <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> is instead <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">determined only by experience</a> derived from <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">sense</a> <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> This is now known as empiricism. An example of Locke's belief in empiricism can be seen in his quote, "whatever I write, as soon as I discover it not to be true, my hand shall be the forwardest to throw it into the fire." This shows the ideology of science in his observations in that something must be capable of being tested repeatedly and that nothing is exempt from being disproven. Challenging the work of others, Locke is said to have established the method of <a href="/wiki/Introspection" title="Introspection">introspection</a>, or observing the emotions and behaviours of one's self.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_work">Life and work</span></h2>
<p>Locke's father, also called John, was an attorney who served as clerk to the Justices of the Peace in <a href="/wiki/Chew_Magna" title="Chew Magna">Chew Magna</a><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and as a captain of cavalry for the <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Parliamentarian</a> forces during the early part of the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>. His mother was Agnes Keene. Both parents were <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritans</a>. Locke was born on 29 August 1632, in a small thatched <a href="/wiki/Cottage" title="Cottage">cottage</a> by the church in <a href="/wiki/Wrington" title="Wrington">Wrington</a>, Somerset, about 12 miles from <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>. He was <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptised</a> the same day. Soon after Locke's birth, the family moved to the <a href="/wiki/Market_town" title="Market town">market town</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pensford" title="Pensford">Pensford</a>, about seven miles south of Bristol, where Locke grew up in a rural <a href="/wiki/Tudor_architecture" title="Tudor architecture">Tudor</a> house in <a href="/wiki/Belluton" title="Belluton">Belluton</a>.
</p><p>In 1647, Locke was sent to the prestigious <a href="/wiki/Westminster_School" title="Westminster School">Westminster School</a> in London under the sponsorship of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Popham" title="Alexander Popham">Alexander Popham</a>, a member of Parliament and his father's former commander. After completing studies there, he was admitted to <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church,_Oxford" title="Christ Church, Oxford">Christ Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">Oxford</a>, in the autumn of 1652 at the age of twenty. The dean of the college at the time was <a href="/wiki/John_Owen_(theologian)" title="John Owen (theologian)">John Owen</a>, vice-chancellor of the university. Although a capable student, Locke was irritated by the undergraduate curriculum of the time. He found the works of modern philosophers, such as <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, more interesting than the <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classical</a> material taught at the university. Through his friend <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lower_(physician)" title="Richard Lower (physician)">Richard Lower</a>, whom he knew from the Westminster School, Locke was introduced to medicine and the experimental philosophy being pursued at other universities and in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>, of which he eventually became a member.
</p><p>Locke was awarded a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor&#39;s degree">bachelor's degree</a> in February 1656 and a master's degree in June 1658.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> He obtained a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Bachelor of medicine">bachelor of medicine</a> in February 1675,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002116_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002116-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> having studied medicine extensively during his time at Oxford and worked with such noted scientists and thinkers as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Willis" title="Thomas Willis">Thomas Willis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lower_(physician)" title="Richard Lower (physician)">Richard Lower</a>. In 1666, he met  <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ashley_Cooper,_1st_Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Anthony Ashley Cooper, 1st Earl of Shaftesbury">Anthony Ashley Cooper, Lord Ashley</a>, who had come to Oxford seeking treatment for a <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> infection. Ashley was impressed with Locke and persuaded him to become part of his retinue.
</p><p>Locke had been looking for a career and in 1667 moved into Ashley's home at Exeter House in London, to serve as hIs personal physician. In London, Locke resumed his medical studies under the tutelage of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sydenham" title="Thomas Sydenham">Thomas Sydenham</a>. Sydenham had a major effect on Locke's natural philosophical thinking&#160;&#8211;&#32;an effect that would become evident in <i><a href="/wiki/An_Essay_Concerning_Human_Understanding" title="An Essay Concerning Human Understanding">An Essay Concerning Human Understanding</a>.</i>
</p><p>Locke's medical knowledge was put to the test when Ashley's liver infection became life-threatening. Locke coordinated the advice of several physicians and was probably instrumental in persuading Ashley to undergo surgery (then life-threatening itself) to remove the cyst. Ashley survived and prospered, crediting Locke with saving his life.
</p><p>During this time, Locke served as Secretary of the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Trade" title="Board of Trade">Board of Trade</a> and Plantations and Secretary to the <a href="/wiki/Lords_Proprietor" class="mw-redirect" title="Lords Proprietor">Lords Proprietors</a> of Carolina, which helped to shape his ideas on international trade and economics.
</p><p>Ashley, as a founder of the <a href="/wiki/British_Whig_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="British Whig Party">Whig</a> movement, exerted great influence on Locke's political ideas. Locke became involved in politics when Ashley became <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a> in 1672 (Ashley being created <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Earl of Shaftesbury">1st Earl of Shaftesbury</a> in 1673). Following Shaftesbury's fall from favour in 1675, Locke spent some time travelling across France as tutor and medical attendant to <a href="/wiki/Caleb_Banks" title="Caleb Banks">Caleb Banks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Henning_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henning-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He returned to England in 1679 when Shaftesbury's political fortunes took a brief positive turn. Around this time, most likely at Shaftesbury's prompting, Locke composed the bulk of the <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a></i>. While it was once thought that Locke wrote the <i>Treatises</i> to defend the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> of 1688, recent scholarship has shown that the work was composed well before this date.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaslett1988III._&#39;&#39;Two_Treatises_of_Government&#39;&#39;_and_the_Revolution_of_1688_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaslett1988III._&#39;&#39;Two_Treatises_of_Government&#39;&#39;_and_the_Revolution_of_1688-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The work is now viewed as a more general argument against <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a> (particularly as espoused by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Filmer" title="Robert Filmer">Robert Filmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>) and for individual consent as the basis of <a href="/wiki/Political_legitimacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Political legitimacy">political legitimacy</a>. Although Locke was associated with the influential Whigs, his ideas about <a href="/wiki/Natural_and_legal_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural and legal rights">natural rights</a> and government are today considered quite revolutionary for that period in English history.
</p><p>Locke fled to the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> in 1683, under strong suspicion of involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Rye_House_Plot" title="Rye House Plot">Rye House Plot</a>, although there is little evidence to suggest that he was directly involved in the scheme. The philosopher and novelist <a href="/wiki/Rebecca_Newberger_Goldstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Rebecca Newberger Goldstein">Rebecca Newberger Goldstein</a> argues that during his five years in Holland, Locke chose his friends "from among the same freethinking members of dissenting Protestant groups as <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Spinoza's</a> small group of loyal confidants. [Baruch Spinoza had died in 1677.] Locke almost certainly met men in Amsterdam who spoke of the ideas of that renegade Jew who... insisted on identifying himself through his religion of reason alone." While she says that "Locke's strong empiricist tendencies" would have "disinclined him to read a grandly metaphysical work such as Spinoza's <i><a href="/wiki/Ethics_(Spinoza)" title="Ethics (Spinoza)">Ethics</a></i>, in other ways he was deeply receptive to Spinoza's ideas, most particularly to the rationalist's well thought out argument for political and <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious tolerance">religious tolerance</a> and the necessity of the separation of church and state."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Netherlands, Locke had time to return to his writing, spending a great deal of time re-working the <i>Essay</i> and composing the <i>Letter on Toleration.</i> Locke did not return home until after the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>. Locke accompanied <a href="/wiki/Mary_II_of_England" title="Mary II of England">Mary II</a> back to England in 1688. The bulk of Locke's publishing took place upon his return from exile&#160;&#8211;&#32;his aforementioned <i>Essay Concerning Human Understanding</i>, the <i>Two Treatises of Civil Government</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Letter_Concerning_Toleration" title="A Letter Concerning Toleration">A Letter Concerning Toleration</a></i> all appearing in quick succession.
</p><p>Locke's close friend <a href="/wiki/Lady_Masham" class="mw-redirect" title="Lady Masham">Lady Masham</a> invited him to join her at Otes, the Mashams' country house in Essex. Although his time there was marked by variable health from <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> attacks, he nevertheless became an intellectual hero of the Whigs. During this period he discussed matters with such figures as <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">John Dryden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>.
</p><p>He died on 28 October 1704, and is buried in the churchyard of the village of <a href="/wiki/High_Laver" title="High Laver">High Laver</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> east of <a href="/wiki/Harlow" title="Harlow">Harlow</a> in Essex, where he had lived in the household of Sir Francis Masham since 1691. Locke never married nor had children.
</p><p>Events that happened during Locke's lifetime include the <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">English Restoration</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plague_of_London" title="Great Plague of London">Great Plague of London</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_London" title="Great Fire of London">Great Fire of London</a>. He did not quite see the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1707" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1707">Act of Union of 1707</a>, though the thrones of England and Scotland were held in <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> throughout his lifetime. <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">Constitutional monarchy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_democracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary democracy">parliamentary democracy</a> were in their infancy during Locke's time.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideas">Ideas</span></h2>
<p>In the late 17th and early 18th centuries, Locke's <i>Two Treatises</i> were rarely cited. Historian Julian Hoppit said of the book, "except among some Whigs, even as a contribution to the intense debate of the 1690s it made little impression and was generally ignored until 1703 (though in Oxford in 1695 it was reported to have made 'a great noise')".<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Philipps_Kenyon" title="John Philipps Kenyon">John Kenyon</a>, in his study of British political debate from 1689 to 1720, has remarked that Locke's theories were "mentioned so rarely in the early stages of the [Glorious] Revolution, up to 1692, and even less thereafter, unless it was to heap abuse on them" and that "no one, including most Whigs, [were] ready for the idea of a notional or abstract contract of the kind adumbrated by Locke".<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, Kenyon adds that <a href="/wiki/Algernon_Sidney" title="Algernon Sidney">Algernon Sidney</a>'s <i>Discourses Concerning Government</i> were "certainly much more influential than Locke's <i>Two Treatises</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Locke-John-LOC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Locke-John-LOC.jpg/220px-Locke-John-LOC.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Locke-John-LOC.jpg/330px-Locke-John-LOC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Locke-John-LOC.jpg/440px-Locke-John-LOC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="875" data-file-height="1140" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Locke-John-LOC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Locke</div></div></div>
<p>In the 50 years after Queen Anne's death in 1714, the <i>Two Treatises</i> were reprinted only once (except in the collected works of Locke). However, with the rise of American resistance to British taxation, the <i>Second Treatise</i> gained a new readership; it was frequently cited in the debates in both America and Britain. The first American printing occurred in 1773 in Boston.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Locke exercised a profound influence on political philosophy, in particular on modern liberalism. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Zuckert" title="Michael Zuckert">Michael Zuckert</a> has argued that Locke launched liberalism by tempering Hobbesian absolutism and clearly <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separating the realms of Church and State</a>. He had a strong influence on <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> who called him "<i>le sage</i> Locke". His arguments concerning <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">liberty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> later influenced the written works of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers of the United States</a>. In fact, one passage from the <i>Second Treatise</i> is reproduced verbatim in the Declaration of Independence, the reference to a "long train of abuses". Such was Locke's influence that Thomas Jefferson wrote: "<a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Bacon</a>, Locke and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a>... I consider them as the three greatest men that have ever lived, without any exception, and as having laid the foundation of those superstructures which have been raised in the Physical and Moral sciences".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>But Locke's influence may have been even more profound in the realm of epistemology. Locke redefined subjectivity, or self, and intellectual historians such as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Taylor_(philosopher)" title="Charles Taylor (philosopher)">Charles Taylor</a> and Jerrold Seigel argue that Locke's <i><a href="/wiki/An_Essay_Concerning_Human_Understanding" title="An Essay Concerning Human Understanding">An Essay Concerning Human Understanding</a></i> (1690) marks the beginning of the modern Western conception of the self.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Locke's theory of association heavily influenced the subject matter of modern psychology. At the time, the empiricist philosopher's recognition of two types of ideas, <i>simple</i> and <i>complex</i> ideas, more importantly their interaction through associationism inspired other philosophers, such as <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">George Berkeley</a>, to revise and expand this theory and apply it to explain how humans gain knowledge in the physical world.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_of_religious_tolerance">Theories of religious tolerance</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Toleration#Locke" title="Toleration">Toleration §&#160;Locke</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg/220px-John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg/330px-John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg/440px-John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2056" data-file-height="3088" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Locke by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Westmacott" title="Richard Westmacott">Richard Westmacott</a>, University College, London</div></div></div>
<p>Locke, writing his <i>Letters Concerning Toleration</i> (1689–1692) in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/European_wars_of_religion" title="European wars of religion">European wars of religion</a>, formulated a classic reasoning for religious tolerance. Three arguments are central: (1) Earthly judges, the state in particular, and human beings generally, cannot dependably evaluate the truth-claims of competing religious standpoints; (2) Even if they could, enforcing a single "true religion" would not have the desired effect, because belief cannot be compelled by violence; (3) Coercing religious uniformity would lead to more social disorder than allowing diversity.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With regard to his position on religious tolerance, Locke was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Baptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptist">Baptist</a> theologians like <a href="/wiki/John_Smyth_(Baptist_minister)" title="John Smyth (Baptist minister)">John Smyth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Helwys" title="Thomas Helwys">Thomas Helwys</a>, who had published tracts demanding freedom of conscience in the early 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlmstead196018_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlmstead196018-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>  Baptist theologian <a href="/wiki/Roger_Williams_(theologian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Roger Williams (theologian)">Roger Williams</a> founded the colony <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a> in 1636, where he combined a democratic constitution with unlimited religious freedom. His tract <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bloody_Tenent_of_Persecution_for_Cause_of_Conscience" class="mw-redirect" title="The Bloody Tenent of Persecution for Cause of Conscience">The Bloody Tenent of Persecution for Cause of Conscience</a></i> (1644), which was widely read in the mother country, was a passionate plea for absolute religious freedom and the total separation of church and state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlmstead1960102–05_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlmstead1960102–05-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>  Freedom of conscience had had high priority on the theological, philosophical and political agenda, since <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> refused to recant his beliefs before the Diet of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> at <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Worms" title="Diet of Worms">Worms</a> in 1521, unless he would be proved false by the Bible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlmstead19605_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlmstead19605-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitution_of_Carolina">Constitution of Carolina</span></h3>
<p>Appraisals of Locke have often been tied to appraisals of liberalism in general, and to appraisals of the United States. Detractors note that (in 1671) he was a major investor in the English slave-trade through the <a href="/wiki/Royal_African_Company" title="Royal African Company">Royal African Company</a>. In addition, he participated in drafting the <i><a href="/wiki/Fundamental_Constitutions_of_Carolina" title="Fundamental Constitutions of Carolina">Fundamental Constitutions of Carolina</a></i> while <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ashley-Cooper,_1st_Earl_of_Shaftesbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthony Ashley-Cooper, 1st Earl of Shaftesbury">Shaftesbury</a>'s secretary, which established a feudal aristocracy and gave a master absolute power over his slaves. For example, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Cohen_(philosopher)" title="Martin Cohen (philosopher)">Martin Cohen</a> notes that Locke, as a secretary to the Council of Trade and Plantations (1673–1674) and a member of the Board of Trade (1696–1700), was in fact, "one of just half a dozen men who created and supervised both the colonies and their iniquitous systems of servitude".<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Some see his statements on <a href="/wiki/Enclosure" title="Enclosure">unenclosed</a> <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">property</a> as having been intended to justify the displacement of the Native Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>  Because of his opposition to aristocracy and slavery in his major writings, he is accused of hypocrisy and racism, or of caring only for the liberty of English capitalists.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Locke also drafted implementing instructions for the Carolina colonists designed to ensure that settlement and development was consistent with the Fundamental Constitutions. Collectively, these documents are known as the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Model_for_the_Province_of_Carolina" title="Grand Model for the Province of Carolina">Grand Model for the Province of Carolina</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_of_value_and_property">Theory of value and property</span></h3>
<p>Locke uses the word <i>property</i> in both broad and narrow senses. In a broad sense, it covers a wide range of human interests and aspirations; more narrowly, it refers to material goods. He argues that property is a natural right and it is derived from <a href="/wiki/Manual_labour" title="Manual labour">labour</a>. In Chapter V of his <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Second Treatise</a></i>, Locke argues that the individual ownership of goods and property is justified by the labour exerted to produce those goods - “at least where there is enough [land], and as good, left in common for others” (paragraph 27) - or utilise property to produce goods beneficial to human society.<sup id="cite_ref-labortheory_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-labortheory-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Locke stated his belief, in his <i>Second Treatise</i>, that nature on its own provides little of value to society, implying that the labour expended in the creation of goods gives them their value. This position can be seen as a <a href="/wiki/Labour_theory_of_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour theory of value">labour theory of value</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-labortheory_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-labortheory-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> From this premise, Locke developed a <a href="/wiki/Labour_theory_of_property" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour theory of property">labour theory of property</a>, namely that ownership of <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">property</a> is created by the application of labour. In addition, he believed that property precedes government and government cannot "dispose of the estates of the subjects arbitrarily." <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> later critiqued Locke's theory of property in his own social theory.
</p>

<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:transparent;border-top:1px solid #aaa;text-align:center;">Related topics</div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Political_views_of_American_academics" title="Political views of American academics">Bias in academia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Media_bias" title="Media bias">Bias in the media</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:Yellow_flag_waving.svg" class="image"><img alt="Yellow flag waving.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/15px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/22px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/30px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="249" data-file-height="268" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Liberalism" title="Portal:Liberalism">Liberalism&#32;portal</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg" class="image"><img alt="A coloured voting box.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Liberalism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Locke's political theory was founded on <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> theory. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, Locke believed that <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a> is characterised by reason and tolerance. Like Hobbes, Locke believed that human nature allowed people to be selfish. This is apparent with the introduction of currency. In a <a href="/wiki/State_of_nature#Locke&#39;s_view_on_the_state_of_nature" title="State of nature">natural state</a> all people were equal and independent, and everyone had a natural right to defend his "life, health, liberty, or possessions".<sup id="cite_ref-locke_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-locke-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>198</span></sup> Most scholars trace the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Life,_Liberty_and_the_pursuit_of_Happiness" title="Life, Liberty and the pursuit of Happiness">Life, Liberty and the pursuit of Happiness</a>," in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">American Declaration of Independence</a>, to Locke's theory of rights,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> though other origins have been suggested.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Like Hobbes, Locke assumed that the sole right to defend in the state of nature was not enough, so people established a <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civil society</a> to resolve conflicts in a civil way with help from government in a state of society. However, Locke never refers to Hobbes by name and may instead have been responding to other writers of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Locke also advocated governmental <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of powers</a> and believed that revolution is not only a <a href="/wiki/Right_of_revolution" title="Right of revolution">right</a> but an obligation in some circumstances. These ideas would come to have profound influence on the Declaration of Independence and the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" title="Constitution of the United States">Constitution of the United States</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Limits_to_accumulation">Limits to accumulation</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lockean_proviso" title="Lockean proviso">Lockean proviso</a></div>
<p>According to Locke, unused property is wasteful and an offence against nature,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>  but, with the introduction of "durable" goods, men could exchange their excessive perishable goods for goods that would last longer and thus not offend the <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a>. In his view, the introduction of money marks the culmination of this process, making possible the unlimited accumulation of property without causing waste through spoilage.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>  He also includes gold or silver as money because they may be "hoarded up without injury to anyone,"<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> since they do not spoil or decay in the hands of the possessor. In his view, the introduction of money eliminates the limits of accumulation. Locke stresses that inequality has come about by tacit agreement on the use of money, not by the social contract establishing civil society or the law of land regulating property. Locke is aware of a problem posed by unlimited accumulation but does not consider it his task. He just implies that government would function to moderate the conflict between the unlimited accumulation of property and a more nearly equal distribution of wealth; he does not identify which principles that government should apply to solve this problem. However, not all elements of his thought form a consistent whole. For example, <a href="/wiki/Labour_theory_of_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour theory of value">labour theory of value</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a></i> stands side by side with the demand-and-supply theory developed in a letter he wrote titled <i>Some Considerations on the Consequences of the Lowering of Interest and the Raising of the Value of Money</i>. Moreover, Locke anchors property in labour but in the end upholds the unlimited accumulation of wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_price_theory">On price theory</span></h3>
<p>Locke's general theory of value and price is a <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply and demand</a> theory, which was set out in a letter to a Member of Parliament in 1691, titled <i>Some Considerations on the Consequences of the Lowering of Interest and the Raising of the Value of Money</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> He refers to supply as "quantity" and demand as "<a href="/wiki/Economic_rent" title="Economic rent">rent</a>". "The price of any commodity rises or falls by the proportion of the number of buyers and sellers," and "that which regulates the price... [of goods] is nothing else but their quantity in proportion to their rent." The quantity theory of money forms a special case of this general theory. His idea is based on "money answers all things" (Ecclesiastes) or "rent of money is always sufficient, or more than enough," and "varies very little..." Locke concludes that as far as money is concerned, the demand is exclusively regulated by its quantity, regardless of whether the <a href="/wiki/Demand_for_money" title="Demand for money">demand for money</a> is unlimited or constant. He also investigates the determinants of demand and supply. For supply, he explains the value of goods as based on their scarcity and ability to be exchanged and consumed. He explains demand for goods as based on their ability to yield a flow of income. Locke develops an early theory of <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capitalisation</a>, such as land, which has value because "by its constant production of saleable commodities it brings in a certain yearly income." He considers the demand for money as almost the same as demand for goods or land; it depends on whether money is wanted as medium of exchange. As a medium of exchange, he states that "money is capable by exchange to procure us the necessaries or conveniences of life," and for loanable funds, "it comes to be of the same nature with land by yielding a certain yearly income... or interest."
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Monetary_thoughts">Monetary thoughts</span></h4>
<p>Locke distinguishes two functions of money, as a "counter" to measure value, and as a "pledge" to lay claim to <a href="/wiki/Good_(economics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Good (economics)">goods</a>. He believes that silver and gold, as opposed to paper money, are the appropriate currency for international transactions. Silver and gold, he says, are treated to have equal value by all of humanity and can thus be treated as a pledge by anyone, while the value of paper money is only valid under the government which issues it.
</p><p>Locke argues that a country should seek a favourable <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_trade" title="Balance of trade">balance of trade</a>, lest it fall behind other countries and suffer a loss in its trade. Since the world money stock grows constantly, a country must constantly seek to enlarge its own stock. Locke develops his theory of foreign exchanges, in addition to commodity movements, there are also movements in country stock of money, and movements of capital determine exchange rates. He considers the latter less significant and less volatile than commodity movements. As for a country's <a href="/wiki/Money_supply" title="Money supply">money stock</a>, if it is large relative to that of other countries, he says it will cause the country's exchange to rise above par, as an export balance would do.
</p><p>He also prepares estimates of the <a href="/wiki/Cash" title="Cash">cash</a> requirements for different economic groups (landholders, labourers and brokers). In each group he posits that the cash requirements are closely related to the length of the pay period. He argues the brokers&#160;– middlemen&#160;– whose activities enlarge the monetary circuit and whose profits eat into the earnings of labourers and landholders, have a negative influence on both personal and the public economy to which they supposedly contribute.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_self">The self</span></h3>
<p>Locke defines the self as "that conscious thinking thing, (whatever substance, made up of whether spiritual, or material, simple, or compounded, it matters not) which is sensible, or conscious of pleasure and pain, capable of happiness or misery, and so is concerned for itself, as far as that consciousness extends".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELocke1997307_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELocke1997307-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>  He does not, however, ignore "substance", writing that "the body too goes to the making the man."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELocke1997306_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELocke1997306-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his <i>Essay</i>, Locke explains the gradual unfolding of this conscious mind. Arguing against both the <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustinian</a> view of man as <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">originally sinful</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">Cartesian</a> position, which holds that man innately knows basic logical propositions, Locke posits an "empty" mind, a <i>tabula rasa</i>, which is shaped by experience; <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sensation" class="extiw" title="wikt:sensation">sensations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_self-reflection" class="mw-redirect" title="Human self-reflection">reflections</a> being the two sources of <b>all</b> our <a href="/wiki/Idea" title="Idea">ideas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Locke's <i><a href="/wiki/Some_Thoughts_Concerning_Education" title="Some Thoughts Concerning Education">Some Thoughts Concerning Education</a></i> is an outline on how to educate this mind: he expresses the belief that education maketh the man, or, more fundamentally, that the mind is an "empty cabinet", with the statement, "I think I may say that of all the men we meet with, nine parts of ten are what they are, good or evil, useful or not, by their education."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELocke199610_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELocke199610-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Locke also wrote that "the little and almost insensible impressions on our tender infancies have very important and lasting consequences."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELocke199610_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELocke199610-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>  He argued that the "<a href="/wiki/Association_of_Ideas" class="mw-redirect" title="Association of Ideas">associations of ideas</a>" that one makes when young are more important than those made later because they are the foundation of the self: they are, put differently, what first mark the <i>tabula rasa</i>. In his <i>Essay</i>, in which both these concepts are introduced, Locke warns against, for example, letting "a foolish maid" convince a child that "goblins and sprites" are associated with the night for "darkness shall ever afterwards bring with it those frightful ideas, and they shall be so joined, that he can no more bear the one than the other."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELocke1997357_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELocke1997357-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This theory came to be called "associationism", and it strongly influenced 18th-century thought, particularly <a href="/wiki/Education_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Education theory">educational theory</a>, as nearly every educational writer warned parents not to allow their children to develop negative associations. It also led to the development of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> and other new disciplines with <a href="/wiki/David_Hartley_(philosopher)" title="David Hartley (philosopher)">David Hartley</a>'s attempt to discover a biological mechanism for associationism in his <i><a href="/wiki/Observations_on_Man" title="Observations on Man">Observations on Man</a></i> (1749).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dream_argument">Dream argument</span></h3>
<p>Locke was critical of Descartes' version of the <a href="/wiki/Dream_argument" title="Dream argument">dream argument</a>, with Locke making the counterargument that people cannot have physical pain in dreams as they do in waking life.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_beliefs">Religious beliefs</span></h2>
<p>Some scholars have seen Locke's political convictions as being based from his religious beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Locke's religious trajectory began in <a href="/wiki/Calvinist" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> <a href="/wiki/Trinitarianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinitarianism">trinitarianism</a>, but by the time of the <i>Reflections</i> (1695) Locke was advocating not just <a href="/wiki/Socinianism" title="Socinianism">Socinian</a> views on tolerance but also Socinian Christology.<sup id="cite_ref-Marshall_1994_426_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall_1994_426-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> However Wainwright (1987) notes that in the posthumously published <i>Paraphrase</i> (1707) Locke's interpretation of one verse, Ephesians 1:10, is markedly different from that of Socinians like <a href="/wiki/John_Biddle_(Unitarian)" title="John Biddle (Unitarian)">Biddle</a>, and may indicate that near the end of his life Locke returned nearer to an <a href="/wiki/Arian" class="mw-redirect" title="Arian">Arian</a> position, thereby accepting Christ's pre-existence.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marshall_1994_426_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall_1994_426-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
Locke was at times not sure about the subject of <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">original sin</a>, so he was accused of Socinianism, Arianism, or Deism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200227,_223_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200227,_223-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>  But he did not deny the reality of evil. Man was capable of waging unjust wars and committing crimes. Criminals had to be punished, even with the death penalty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002145_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002145-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>  With regard to the Bible Locke was very conservative. He retained the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Verbal_inspiration" class="mw-redirect" title="Verbal inspiration">verbal inspiration</a> of the Scriptures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>  The miracles were proof of the divine nature of the biblical message. Locke was convinced that the entire content of the Bible was in agreement with human reason (<i>The reasonableness of Christianity</i>, 1695).<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>  Although Locke was an advocate of tolerance, he urged the authorities not to tolerate <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a>, because he thought the denial of God's existence would undermine the social order and lead to chaos.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002217_ff_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002217_ff-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>  That excluded all atheistic varieties of philosophy and all attempts to deduce ethics and natural law from purely secular premises.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200213_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200213-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>  In Locke's opinion the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_argument" title="Cosmological argument">cosmological argument</a> was valid and proved God's existence. His political thought was based on Protestant Christian views.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200213_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200213-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, Locke advocated a sense of piety out of gratitude to God for giving reason to men.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_From_Religion">Philosophy From Religion</span></h3>
<p>Locke's concept of man started with the belief in creation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002142_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002142-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>  Like philosophers <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pufendorf" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuel Pufendorf">Samuel Pufendorf</a>, Locke equated <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a> with the biblical <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200212_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200212-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Locke derived the fundamental concepts of his political theory from biblical texts, in particular from <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Genesis</a> 1 and 2 (<a href="/wiki/Genesis_creation_narrative" title="Genesis creation narrative">creation</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Decalogue</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Rule" title="Golden Rule">Golden Rule</a>, the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>, and the letters of <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200222–43,_45–46,_101,_153–58,_195,_197_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200222–43,_45–46,_101,_153–58,_195,_197-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">The Decalogue</a> puts a person's life, their reputation, and property under God's protection.
</p><p>Locke's philosophy on freedom is also derived from the Bible. Locke also derived basic human equality from the Bible, including the <a href="/wiki/Equality_of_the_sexes" class="mw-redirect" title="Equality of the sexes">equality of the sexes</a>, the starting point of the theological doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Imago_Dei" class="mw-redirect" title="Imago Dei">Imago Dei</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200221–43_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200221–43-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>  To Locke, one of the consequences of the principle of equality was that all humans were created equally free and therefore governments needed the consent of the governed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002136_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002136-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Locke compared the English Monarchy's rule over the British people to Adam's rule over Eve in Genesis, which was appointed by God.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following Locke's philosophy, the American <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> founded <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> partially on the biblical belief in creation. Locke's doctrine that governments need the consent of the governed is also central to the Declaration of Independence.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047268">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{list-style-type:none;margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>dl>dd{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em;list-style:none}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-100{font-size:100%}</style><div class="refbegin columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Richard_Ashcraft" title="Richard Ashcraft">Ashcraft, Richard</a>, 1986.  <i>Revolutionary Politics &amp; Locke's Two Treatises of Government.</i>  Princeton: Princeton University Press. Discusses the relationship between Locke's philosophy and his political activities.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Michael_R._Ayers" title="Michael R. Ayers">Ayers, Michael</a>, 1991. <i>Locke. Epistemology &amp; Ontology</i> Routledge (the standard work on Locke's Essay Concerning Human Understanding.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bernard_Bailyn" title="Bernard Bailyn">Bailyn, Bernard</a>, 1992 (1967). <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ideological_Origins_of_the_American_Revolution" title="The Ideological Origins of the American Revolution">The Ideological Origins of the American Revolution</a></i>. Harvard Uni. Press. Discusses the influence of Locke and other thinkers upon the American Revolution and on subsequent American political thought.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gerald_Cohen" class="mw-redirect" title="Gerald Cohen">Cohen, Gerald</a>, 1995. 'Marx and Locke on Land and Labour', in his <i>Self-Ownership, Freedom and Equality</i>, Oxford University Press.</li>
<li>Cox, Richard, <i>Locke on War and Peace</i>, Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1960. A discussion of Locke's theory of international relations.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vere_Claiborne_Chappell" title="Vere Claiborne Chappell">Chappell, Vere</a>, ed., 1994. <i>The Cambridge Companion to Locke</i>. Cambridge U.P. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.amazon.com/Cambridge-Companion-Locke-Companions-Philosophy/dp/0521387728/">excerpt and text search</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Dunn_(political_theorist)" title="John Dunn (political theorist)">Dunn, John</a>, 1984. <i>Locke</i>. Oxford Uni. Press. A succinct introduction.</li>
<li>———, 1969. <i>The Political Thought of John Locke: An Historical Account of the Argument of the "Two Treatises of Government"</i>. Cambridge Uni. Press. Introduced the interpretation which emphasises the theological element in Locke's political thought.</li>
<li>Hudson, Nicholas, "John Locke and the Tradition of Nominalism," in: <i>Nominalism and Literary Discourse</i>, ed. Hugo Keiper, <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Bode" title="Christoph Bode">Christoph Bode</a>, and Richard Utz (Amsterdam: Rodopi, 1997), pp.&#160;283–99.</li>
<li><cite class="citation encyclopaedia">Mack, Eric (2008). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=yxNgXs3TkJYC">"Locke, John (1632–1704)"</a>.  In <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Hamowy" title="Ronald Hamowy">Hamowy, Ronald</a> (ed.). <i>The Encyclopedia of Libertarianism</i>. Thousand Oaks, CA: <a href="/wiki/SAGE_Publications" class="mw-redirect" title="SAGE Publications">Sage</a>; <a href="/wiki/Cato_Institute" title="Cato Institute">Cato Institute</a>. pp.&#160;305–07. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.4135%2F9781412965811.n184">10.4135/9781412965811.n184</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1412965804" title="Special:BookSources/978-1412965804"><bdi>978-1412965804</bdi></a>. <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_Control_Number" title="Library of Congress Control Number">LCCN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//lccn.loc.gov/2008009151">2008009151</a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC" title="OCLC">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/750831024">750831024</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Locke%2C+John+%281632%E2%80%931704%29&amp;rft.btitle=The+Encyclopedia+of+Libertarianism&amp;rft.place=Thousand+Oaks%2C+CA&amp;rft.pages=305-07&amp;rft.pub=Sage%3B+Cato+Institute&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F750831024&amp;rft_id=info%3Alccn%2F2008009151&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.4135%2F9781412965811.n184&amp;rft.isbn=978-1412965804&amp;rft.aulast=Mack&amp;rft.aufirst=Eric&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DyxNgXs3TkJYC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Locke" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/C._B._Macpherson" title="C. B. Macpherson">Macpherson, C.B.</a> <i>The Political Theory of Possessive Individualism: Hobbes to Locke</i> (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1962). Establishes the deep affinity from Hobbes to Harrington, the Levellers, and Locke through to nineteenth-century utilitarianism.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFMoseley2007" class="citation">Moseley, Alexander (2007), <i>John Locke: Continuum Library of Educational Thought</i>, Continuum, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8264-8405-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8264-8405-5"><bdi>978-0-8264-8405-5</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=John+Locke%3A+Continuum+Library+of+Educational+Thought&amp;rft.pub=Continuum&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8264-8405-5&amp;rft.aulast=Moseley&amp;rft.aufirst=Alexander&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Locke" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pangle" title="Thomas Pangle">Pangle, Thomas</a>, <i>The Spirit of Modern Republicanism: The Moral Vision of the American Founders and the Philosophy of Locke</i> (Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 1988; paperback ed., 1990), 334 pp.. Challenges Dunn's, Tully's, Yolton's, and other conventional readings.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFRobinsonGroves2003" class="citation">Robinson, Dave; Groves, Judy (2003), <i>Introducing Political Philosophy</i>, Icon Books, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-84046-450-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-84046-450-4"><bdi>978-1-84046-450-4</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Introducing+Political+Philosophy&amp;rft.pub=Icon+Books&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-84046-450-4&amp;rft.aulast=Robinson&amp;rft.aufirst=Dave&amp;rft.au=Groves%2C+Judy&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Locke" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFRousseau2004" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/George_Rousseau" title="George Rousseau">Rousseau, George S.</a> (2004), <i>Nervous Acts: Essays on Literature, Culture and Sensibility</i>, Palgrave Macmillan, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4039-3453-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4039-3453-6"><bdi>978-1-4039-3453-6</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Nervous+Acts%3A+Essays+on+Literature%2C+Culture+and+Sensibility&amp;rft.pub=Palgrave+Macmillan&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4039-3453-6&amp;rft.aulast=Rousseau&amp;rft.aufirst=George+S.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Locke" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Strauss,_Leo" class="mw-redirect" title="Strauss, Leo">Strauss, Leo</a>. <i>Natural Right and History</i>, chap. 5B (Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 1953). Argues from a non-Marxist point of view for a deep affinity between Hobbes and Locke.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFStrauss1958" class="citation">Strauss, Leo (1958), "Critical Note: Locke's Doctrine of Natural Law", <i>The American Political Science Review</i>, <b>52</b> (2): 490–501, <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.2307%2F1952329">10.2307/1952329</a>, <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/1952329">1952329</a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+American+Political+Science+Review&amp;rft.atitle=Critical+Note%3A+Locke%27s+Doctrine+of+Natural+Law&amp;rft.volume=52&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=490-501&amp;rft.date=1958&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F1952329&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F1952329&amp;rft.aulast=Strauss&amp;rft.aufirst=Leo&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Locke" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/>  A critique of W. von Leyden's edition of Locke's unpublished writings on natural law.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Tully_(philosopher)" title="James Tully (philosopher)">Tully, James</a>, 1980.  <i>A Discourse on Property&#160;: John Locke and his Adversaries</i>. Cambridge Uni. Press</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Waldron" title="Jeremy Waldron">Waldron, Jeremy</a>, 2002. <i>God, Locke and Equality</i>. Cambridge Uni. Press.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_W._Yolton" title="John W. Yolton">Yolton, John W.</a>, ed., 1969. <i>John Locke: Problems and Perspectives</i>. Cambridge Uni. Press</li>
<li>Yolton, John W., ed., 1993. <i>A Locke Dictionary</i>. Oxford: Blackwell.</li>
<li>Zuckert, Michael, <i>Launching Liberalism: On Lockean Political Philosophy</i>. Lawrence: University Press of Kansas</li>
<li><i>Locke Studies</i>, appearing annually from 2001, formerly <i>The Locke Newsletter</i> (1970–2000), publishes scholarly work on John Locke.</li></ul>
